<p><b>Alabama</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">AL</span>-ə-<span style="font-size:90%">BAM</span>-ə</i></a>)<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">state</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">Southeastern</a> region of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. It borders <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> to the south, and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> to the west. Alabama is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_area" title="List of U.S. states and territories by area">30th largest by area</a><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_population" title="List of U.S. states and territories by population">24th-most populous</a> of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_and_territories_of_the_United_States" title="List of states and territories of the United States">50 U.S. states</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alabama is nicknamed the <i><a href="/wiki/Northern_flicker" title="Northern flicker">Yellowhammer</a> State</i>, after the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_birds" title="List of U.S. state birds">state bird</a>. Alabama is also known as the "Heart of <a href="/wiki/Dixie" title="Dixie">Dixie</a>" and the "Cotton State". The state has diverse geography, with the north dominated by the mountainous <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Valley" title="Tennessee Valley">Tennessee Valley</a> and the south by <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Bay" title="Mobile Bay">Mobile Bay</a>, a historically significant port. Alabama's capital is <a href="/wiki/Montgomery,_Alabama" title="Montgomery, Alabama">Montgomery</a>, and its largest city by population and area is <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-al.com_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-al.com-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Its oldest city is <a href="/wiki/Mobile,_Alabama" title="Mobile, Alabama">Mobile</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/French_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="French colonization of the Americas">French colonists</a> (<a href="/wiki/Alabama_Creole_people" title="Alabama Creole people">Alabama Creoles</a>) in 1702 as the capital of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_France)" title="Louisiana (New France)">French Louisiana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pelican_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pelican-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-meltonmclaurin_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meltonmclaurin-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_metropolitan_area,_Alabama" title="Birmingham metropolitan area, Alabama">Greater Birmingham</a> is Alabama's largest metropolitan area and its economic center.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Politically, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a>, Alabama is predominantly a <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a> state, and is known for its <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Southern_United_States" title="Culture of the Southern United States">Southern culture</a>. Within Alabama, <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a>, particularly at the <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">college</a> level, plays a major part of the state's culture. 
</p><p>Originally home to many native tribes, present-day Alabama was a Spanish territory beginning in the sixteenth century until the French acquired it in the early eighteenth century. The British won the territory in 1763 until losing it in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. Spain held Mobile as part of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_West_Florida" title="Spanish West Florida">Spanish West Florida</a> until 1813. In December 1819, Alabama was recognized as a state. During the antebellum period, Alabama was a major <a href="/wiki/Cotton_production_in_the_United_States" title="Cotton production in the United States">producer of cotton</a>, and widely used <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African American</a> <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slave</a> labor. In 1861, the state seceded from the United States to become part of the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a>, with Montgomery acting as its first capital, and rejoined the Union in 1868. Following the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, Alabama would suffer decades of economic hardship, in part due to agriculture and a few <a href="/wiki/Cash_crops" class="mw-redirect" title="Cash crops">cash crops</a> being the main driver of the state's economy. Similar to other former slave states, Alabamian legislators employed <a href="/wiki/Jim_Crow_laws" title="Jim Crow laws">Jim Crow laws</a> from the late 19th century up until the 1960s. High-profile events such as the <a href="/wiki/Selma_to_Montgomery_marches" title="Selma to Montgomery marches">Selma to Montgomery marches</a> made the state a major focal point of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> in the 1950s and 1960s.
</p><p>During and after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Alabama grew as the state's <a href="/wiki/Economic_diversity" title="Economic diversity">economy diversified</a> with new industries. In 1960, the establishment of <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> in Huntsville helped boost Alabama's economic growth by developing a local <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a> industry. Alabama's economy in the 21st century is based on automotive, finance, tourism, manufacturing, aerospace, mineral extraction, healthcare, education, retail, and technology.<sup id="cite_ref-alaindustrial_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alaindustrial-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2></div>
<p>The name of the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_River" title="Alabama River">Alabama River</a> and state is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_people" title="Alabama people">Alabama people</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Muskogean_languages" title="Muskogean languages">Muskogean-speaking tribe</a> whose members lived just below the <a href="/wiki/Confluence" title="Confluence">confluence</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Coosa_River" title="Coosa River">Coosa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tallapoosa_River" title="Tallapoosa River">Tallapoosa</a> rivers on the upper reaches of the river.<sup id="cite_ref-Read_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Read-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_language" title="Alabama language">Alabama language</a>, the word for a person of Alabama lineage is <span title="Alabama-language text"><i lang="akz">Albaamo</i></span> (or variously <span title="Alabama-language text"><i lang="akz">Albaama</i></span> or <span title="Alabama-language text"><i lang="akz">Albàamo</i></span> in different dialects; the plural form is <span title="Alabama-language text"><i lang="akz">Albaamaha</i></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The word's spelling varies significantly among historical sources.<sup id="cite_ref-ADAH1_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADAH1-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The first usage appears in three accounts of the <a href="/wiki/Hernando_de_Soto" title="Hernando de Soto">Hernando de Soto</a> expedition of 1540: <a href="/wiki/Inca_Garcilaso_de_la_Vega" title="Inca Garcilaso de la Vega">Garcilaso de la Vega</a> used <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Alibamo</i></span>, while the Knight of Elvas and Rodrigo Ranjel wrote <i>Alibamu</i> and <i>Limamu</i>, respectively, in <a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">transliterations</a> of the term.<sup id="cite_ref-ADAH1_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADAH1-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> As early as 1702, the French called the tribe the <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Alibamon</i></span>, with French maps identifying the river as <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Rivière des Alibamons</i></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Read_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Read-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Other spellings of the name have included <i>Alibamu</i>, <i>Alabamo</i>, <i>Albama</i>, <i>Alebamon</i>, <i>Alibama</i>, <i>Alibamou</i>, <i>Alabamu</i>, and <i>Allibamou</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ADAH1_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADAH1-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wills_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wills-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Griffith_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Griffith-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The use of state names derived from <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">Native American languages</a> is common in the U.S.; an estimated 26 states have names of Native American origin.<sup id="cite_ref-Weiss_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weiss-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sources disagree on the word's meaning. Some scholars suggest the word comes from the Choctaw <span title="Choctaw-language text"><i lang="cho">alba</i></span> (meaning 'plants' or 'weeds') and <span title="Choctaw-language text"><i lang="cho">amo</i></span> (meaning 'to cut', 'to trim', or 'to gather').<sup id="cite_ref-ADAH1_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADAH1-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rogers_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swanton1_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swanton1-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The meaning may have been 'clearers of the thicket'<sup id="cite_ref-Rogers_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> or 'herb gatherers',<sup id="cite_ref-Swanton1_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swanton1-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swanton2_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swanton2-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> referring to clearing land for cultivation<sup id="cite_ref-Wills_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wills-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> or collecting medicinal plants.<sup id="cite_ref-Swanton2_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swanton2-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The state has numerous <a href="/wiki/List_of_place_names_of_Native_American_origin_in_Alabama" class="mw-redirect" title="List of place names of Native American origin in Alabama">place names of Native American origin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An 1842 article in the <i>Jacksonville Republican</i> proposed it meant 'Here We Rest'.<sup id="cite_ref-ADAH1_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADAH1-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> This notion was popularized in the 1850s through the writings of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Beaufort_Meek" title="Alexander Beaufort Meek">Alexander Beaufort Meek</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ADAH1_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADAH1-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Experts in the Muskogean languages have not found any evidence to support such a translation.<sup id="cite_ref-Read_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Read-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ADAH1_20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADAH1-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Alabama" title="History of Alabama">History of Alabama</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pre-European_settlement">Pre-European settlement</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Moundville_Archaeological_Site_Alabama.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Moundville_Archaeological_Site_Alabama.jpg/220px-Moundville_Archaeological_Site_Alabama.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Moundville_Archaeological_Site_Alabama.jpg/330px-Moundville_Archaeological_Site_Alabama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Moundville_Archaeological_Site_Alabama.jpg/440px-Moundville_Archaeological_Site_Alabama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="636" data-file-height="425" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Moundville_Archaeological_Site" title="Moundville Archaeological Site">Moundville Archaeological Site</a> in Hale County. It was occupied by Native Americans of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian culture</a> from 1000 to 1450 CE.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">Indigenous peoples</a> of varying cultures lived in the area for thousands of years before the advent of European colonization. Trade with the northeastern tribes by the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> began during the Burial Mound Period (1000<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>BCE&#160;&#8211;&#32;700<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>CE) and continued until <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">European contact</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimesAlmanac_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimesAlmanac-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The agrarian <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian culture</a> covered most of the state from 1000 to 1600 CE, with one of its major centers built at what is now the <a href="/wiki/Moundville_Archaeological_Site" title="Moundville Archaeological Site">Moundville Archaeological Site</a> in <a href="/wiki/Moundville,_Alabama" title="Moundville, Alabama">Moundville, Alabama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This is the second-largest complex of the classic Middle Mississippian era, after <a href="/wiki/Cahokia" title="Cahokia">Cahokia</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, which was the center of the culture. Analysis of <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">artifacts</a> from <a href="/wiki/Archaeological" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaeological">archaeological</a> excavations at Moundville were the basis of scholars' formulating the characteristics of the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Ceremonial_Complex" title="Southeastern Ceremonial Complex">Southeastern Ceremonial Complex</a> (SECC).<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Contrary to popular belief, the SECC appears to have no direct links to <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerican</a> culture but developed independently. The Ceremonial Complex represents a major component of the religion of the Mississippian peoples; it is one of the primary means by which their religion is understood.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the historical tribes of Native American people living in present-day Alabama at the time of European contact were the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Iroquoian_languages" title="Iroquoian languages">Iroquoian language</a> people; and the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee_language" title="Muscogee language">Muskogean-speaking</a> Alabama (<i>Alibamu</i>), <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw" title="Chickasaw">Chickasaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">Choctaw</a>, Creek, and <a href="/wiki/Coushatta" title="Coushatta">Koasati</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> While part of the same large language family, the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Muskogee tribes</a> developed distinct cultures and languages.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="European_settlement">European settlement</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">New France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_France)" title="Louisiana (New France)">Louisiana (New France)</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">Treaty of Paris (1763)</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_Spain)" title="Louisiana (New Spain)">Louisiana (New Spain)</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Florida" title="West Florida">West Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Reserve_(1763)" title="Indian Reserve (1763)">Indian Reserve (1763)</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">Treaty of Paris (1783)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_West_Florida" title="Spanish West Florida">Spanish West Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Wars" title="Seminole Wars">Seminole Wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_West_Florida" title="Republic of West Florida">Republic of West Florida</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Territory" title="Mississippi Territory">Mississippi Territory</a></div>
<p>The Spanish were the first Europeans to reach Alabama during their exploration of North America in the 16th century. The expedition of Hernando de Soto passed through <a href="/wiki/Mabila" title="Mabila">Mabila</a> and other parts of the state in 1540. More than 160 years later, the French founded the region's first European settlement at <a href="/wiki/Old_Mobile_Site" title="Old Mobile Site">Old Mobile</a> in 1702.<sup id="cite_ref-US50_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US50-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The city was moved to the current site of Mobile in 1711. This area was claimed by the French from 1702 to 1763 as part of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_France)" title="Louisiana (New France)">La Louisiane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-alahisttmln_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alahisttmln-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the French lost to the British in the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years&#39; War">Seven Years' War</a>, it became part of British <a href="/wiki/West_Florida" title="West Florida">West Florida</a> from 1763 to 1783. After the U.S. victory in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, the territory was divided between the United States and Spain. The latter retained control of this western territory from 1783 until the surrender of the Spanish garrison at Mobile to U.S. forces on April 13, 1813.<sup id="cite_ref-alahisttmln_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alahisttmln-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-annexed1_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annexed1-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas Bassett, a <a href="/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution)" title="Loyalist (American Revolution)">loyalist</a> to the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British monarchy</a> during the Revolutionary era, was one of the earliest white settlers in the state outside Mobile. He settled in the <a href="/wiki/Tombigbee_District" title="Tombigbee District">Tombigbee District</a> during the early 1770s.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The district's boundaries were roughly limited to the area within a few miles of the <a href="/wiki/Tombigbee_River" title="Tombigbee River">Tombigbee River</a> and included portions of what is today southern <a href="/wiki/Clarke_County,_Alabama" title="Clarke County, Alabama">Clarke County</a>, northernmost <a href="/wiki/Mobile_County,_Alabama" title="Mobile County, Alabama">Mobile County</a>, and most of <a href="/wiki/Washington_County,_Alabama" title="Washington County, Alabama">Washington County</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-oldsw_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oldsw-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-colonial_mobile_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colonial_mobile-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>What are now <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_County,_Alabama" title="Baldwin County, Alabama">Baldwin</a> and Mobile counties became part of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_West_Florida" title="Spanish West Florida">Spanish West Florida</a> in 1783, part of the independent <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_West_Florida" title="Republic of West Florida">Republic of West Florida</a> in 1810, and finally part of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Territory" title="Mississippi Territory">Mississippi Territory</a> in 1812. Most of what is now the northern two-thirds of Alabama was known as the <a href="/wiki/Yazoo_lands" title="Yazoo lands">Yazoo lands</a> beginning during the British colonial period. It was claimed by the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Georgia" title="Province of Georgia">Province of Georgia</a> from 1767 onwards. Following the Revolutionary War, it remained a part of Georgia, although heavily disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pickett_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pickett-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the exception of the area around Mobile and the Yazoo lands, what is now the lower one-third of Alabama was made part of the Mississippi Territory when it was organized in 1798. The Yazoo lands were added to the territory in 1804, following the <a href="/wiki/Yazoo_land_scandal" title="Yazoo land scandal">Yazoo land scandal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pickett_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pickett-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Spain kept a claim on its former Spanish West Florida territory in what would become the coastal counties until the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a> officially ceded it to the U.S. in 1819.<sup id="cite_ref-annexed1_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annexed1-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="19th_century">19th century</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Organic_act#List_of_organic_acts" title="Organic act">Organic act §&#160;List of organic acts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Territory" title="Alabama Territory">Alabama Territory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_the_Union" title="Admission to the Union">Admission to the Union</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_date_of_admission_to_the_Union" title="List of U.S. states by date of admission to the Union">List of U.S. states by date of admission to the Union</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mississippiterritory.PNG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mississippiterritory.PNG/200px-Mississippiterritory.PNG" decoding="async" width="200" height="180" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mississippiterritory.PNG/300px-Mississippiterritory.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mississippiterritory.PNG/400px-Mississippiterritory.PNG 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="431" /></a><figcaption></figcaption></figure>
<p>Before <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi's admission</a> to statehood on December 10, 1817, the more sparsely settled eastern half of the territory was separated and named the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Territory" title="Alabama Territory">Alabama Territory</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> created the Alabama Territory on March 3, 1817. <a href="/wiki/St._Stephens,_Alabama" title="St. Stephens, Alabama">St. Stephens</a>, now abandoned, served as the territorial capital from 1817 to 1819.<sup id="cite_ref-eoaststephens_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eoaststephens-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alabama was admitted as the 22nd state on December 14, 1819, with Congress selecting <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville</a> as the site for the first <a href="/wiki/Constituent_assembly" title="Constituent assembly">Constitutional Convention</a>. From July<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>5 to August 2, 1819, delegates met to prepare the new state constitution. Huntsville served as temporary capital from 1819 to 1820, when the seat of government moved to <a href="/wiki/Cahaba,_Alabama" title="Cahaba, Alabama">Cahaba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dallas_County,_Alabama" title="Dallas County, Alabama">Dallas County</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cahaba,_Alabama" title="Cahaba, Alabama">Cahaba</a>, now a <a href="/wiki/Ghost_town" title="Ghost town">ghost town</a>, was the first permanent state capital from 1820 to 1825.<sup id="cite_ref-Cahaw_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cahaw-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Alabama_Fever" title="Alabama Fever">Alabama Fever</a> land rush was underway when the state was admitted to the Union, with settlers and land speculators pouring into the state to take advantage of fertile land suitable for cotton cultivation.<sup id="cite_ref-fever_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fever-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-adahtalafvr_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adahtalafvr-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Part of the frontier in the 1820s and 1830s, its constitution provided for universal suffrage for white men.<sup id="cite_ref-SSpaces_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSpaces-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Thornhill_01.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Thornhill_01.jpg/220px-Thornhill_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Thornhill_01.jpg/330px-Thornhill_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Thornhill_01.jpg/440px-Thornhill_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6144" data-file-height="4096" /></a><figcaption>The main house, built in 1833, at <a href="/wiki/Thornhill_(Forkland,_Alabama)" title="Thornhill (Forkland, Alabama)">Thornhill</a> in Greene County. It is a former <a href="/wiki/Black_Belt_(region_of_Alabama)" title="Black Belt (region of Alabama)">Black Belt</a> plantation.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Southeastern planters and traders from the <a href="/wiki/Upper_South" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper South">Upper South</a> brought <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_Alabama" title="History of slavery in Alabama">slaves</a> with them as the cotton <a href="/wiki/List_of_plantations_in_Alabama" title="List of plantations in Alabama">plantations in Alabama</a> expanded. The economy of the central <a href="/wiki/Black_Belt_(region_of_Alabama)" title="Black Belt (region of Alabama)">Black Belt</a> (named for its dark, productive soil) was built around large cotton <a href="/wiki/Plantation_complexes_in_the_Southern_United_States" title="Plantation complexes in the Southern United States">plantations</a> whose owners' wealth grew mainly from slave labor.<sup id="cite_ref-SSpaces_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSpaces-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The area also drew many poor, disenfranchised people who became <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_agriculture" title="Subsistence agriculture">subsistence farmers</a>. Alabama had an estimated population of under 10,000 people in 1810, but it increased to more than 300,000 people by 1830.<sup id="cite_ref-fever_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fever-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Most Native American tribes were <a href="/wiki/Indian_removal" title="Indian removal">completely removed</a> from the state within a few years of the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">Indian Removal Act</a> by Congress in 1830.<sup id="cite_ref-ala_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ala-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1826 to 1846, <a href="/wiki/Tuscaloosa,_Alabama" title="Tuscaloosa, Alabama">Tuscaloosa</a> served as Alabama's capital. On January 30, 1846, the Alabama legislature announced it had voted to move the capital city from Tuscaloosa to Montgomery. The first legislative session in the new capital met in December 1847.<sup id="cite_ref-capitols_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capitols-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> A new capitol building was erected under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Decatur_Button" title="Stephen Decatur Button">Stephen Decatur Button</a> of <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>. The first structure burned down in 1849, but was rebuilt on the same site in 1851. This second capitol building in Montgomery remains to the present day. It was designed by Barachias Holt of <a href="/wiki/Exeter,_Maine" title="Exeter, Maine">Exeter, Maine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-alcatalog_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alcatalog-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-alarchitecture_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alarchitecture-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Civil_War_and_Reconstruction">Civil War and Reconstruction</h4></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_of_Secession" title="Ordinance of Secession">Ordinance of Secession</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alabama_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Alabama in the American Civil War">Alabama in the American Civil War</a></div>
<p>By 1860, the population had increased to 964,201 people, of which nearly half, 435,080, were enslaved African Americans, and 2,690 were <a href="/wiki/Free_people_of_color" title="Free people of color">free people of color</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-adahtmln_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adahtmln-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> On January 11, 1861, Alabama declared its <a href="/wiki/Secession_in_the_United_States" title="Secession in the United States">secession</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a>. After remaining an independent republic for a few days, it joined the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a>. The Confederacy's capital was initially at Montgomery. Alabama was heavily <a href="/wiki/Alabama_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Alabama in the American Civil War">involved in the American Civil War</a>. Although comparatively few battles were fought in the state, Alabama contributed about 120,000 soldiers to the war effort.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Huntsville_Courthouse_Square_1864.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Huntsville_Courthouse_Square_1864.jpg/220px-Huntsville_Courthouse_Square_1864.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Huntsville_Courthouse_Square_1864.jpg/330px-Huntsville_Courthouse_Square_1864.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Huntsville_Courthouse_Square_1864.jpg/440px-Huntsville_Courthouse_Square_1864.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="406" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a> troops occupying Courthouse Square in Huntsville, following its capture and occupation by federal forces in 1864</figcaption></figure>
<p>A company of cavalry soldiers from Huntsville, Alabama, joined <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Bedford_Forrest" title="Nathan Bedford Forrest">Nathan Bedford Forrest</a>'s battalion in <a href="/wiki/Hopkinsville,_Kentucky" title="Hopkinsville, Kentucky">Hopkinsville, Kentucky</a>. The company wore new uniforms with yellow trim on the sleeves, collar and coattails. This led to them being greeted with "Yellowhammer", and the name later was applied to all Alabama troops in the Confederate Army.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alabama's slaves were freed by the 13th Amendment in 1865.<sup id="cite_ref-HistDocs_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistDocs-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Alabama was under military rule from the end of the war in May 1865 until its official restoration to the Union in 1868. From 1867 to 1874, with most white citizens barred temporarily from voting and freedmen enfranchised, many African Americans emerged as political leaders in the state. Alabama was represented in Congress during this period by three African-American congressmen: <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Haralson" title="Jeremiah Haralson">Jeremiah Haralson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_S._Turner" title="Benjamin S. Turner">Benjamin S. Turner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_T._Rapier" title="James T. Rapier">James T. Rapier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-alrecnstrctn_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alrecnstrctn-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the war, the state remained chiefly agricultural, with an economy tied to cotton. During Reconstruction, state legislators ratified a <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Alabama" title="Constitution of Alabama">new state constitution</a> in 1868 which created the state's first public school system and expanded women's rights. Legislators funded numerous public road and railroad projects, although these were plagued with allegations of fraud and <a href="/wiki/Misappropriation" title="Misappropriation">misappropriation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-alrecnstrctn_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alrecnstrctn-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Organized <a href="/wiki/Insurgency" title="Insurgency">insurgent</a>, resistance groups tried to suppress the freedmen and Republicans. These groups included The <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a>, the Pale Faces, <a href="/wiki/Knights_of_the_White_Camelia" title="Knights of the White Camelia">Knights of the White Camellia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_Shirts_(United_States)" title="Red Shirts (United States)">Red Shirts</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/White_League" title="White League">White League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-alrecnstrctn_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alrecnstrctn-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Reconstruction in Alabama ended in 1874, when the Democrats regained control of the legislature and governor's office through an election dominated by fraud and violence. They wrote another constitution in 1875,<sup id="cite_ref-alrecnstrctn_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alrecnstrctn-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and the legislature passed the <a href="/wiki/Blaine_Amendment" title="Blaine Amendment">Blaine Amendment</a>, prohibiting public money from being used to finance religious-affiliated schools.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The same year, legislation was approved that called for <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racially segregated</a> schools.<sup id="cite_ref-jimcrowala_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jimcrowala-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Railroad passenger cars were segregated in 1891.<sup id="cite_ref-jimcrowala_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jimcrowala-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="20th_century">20th century</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_Alabama_skyline_1915.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Birmingham_Alabama_skyline_1915.jpg/220px-Birmingham_Alabama_skyline_1915.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Birmingham_Alabama_skyline_1915.jpg/330px-Birmingham_Alabama_skyline_1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Birmingham_Alabama_skyline_1915.jpg/440px-Birmingham_Alabama_skyline_1915.jpg 2x" data-file-width="449" data-file-height="321" /></a><figcaption>The developing skyline of Birmingham in 1915</figcaption></figure>
<p>The new 1901 Constitution of Alabama included provisions for <a href="/wiki/Voter_registration" title="Voter registration">voter registration</a> that effectively <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement_after_the_Reconstruction_era" title="Disfranchisement after the Reconstruction era">disenfranchised</a> large portions of the population, including nearly all African Americans and Native Americans, and tens of thousands of poor European Americans, through making voter registration difficult, requiring a <a href="/wiki/Poll_taxes_in_the_United_States" title="Poll taxes in the United States">poll tax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Literacy_test" title="Literacy test">literacy test</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The 1901 constitution required racial segregation of public schools. By 1903 only 2,980 African Americans were registered in Alabama, although at least 74,000 were <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literate</a>. This compared to more than 181,000 African Americans eligible to vote in 1900. The numbers dropped even more in later decades.<sup id="cite_ref-epzzsd_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epzzsd-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The state legislature passed additional racial segregation laws related to public facilities into the 1950s: jails were segregated in 1911; hospitals in 1915; toilets, hotels, and restaurants in 1928; and bus stop waiting rooms in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-jimcrowala_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jimcrowala-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the planter class had persuaded poor whites to vote for this legislative effort to suppress black voting, the new restrictions resulted in their disenfranchisement as well, due mostly to the imposition of a cumulative poll tax.<sup id="cite_ref-epzzsd_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epzzsd-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> By 1941, whites constituted a slight majority of those disenfranchised by these laws: 600,000 whites vs. 520,000 African Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-epzzsd_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epzzsd-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Nearly all Blacks had lost the ability to vote. Despite numerous legal challenges which succeeded in overturning certain provisions, the state legislature would create new ones to maintain disenfranchisement. The exclusion of blacks from the political system persisted until after passage of federal civil rights legislation in 1965 to enforce their constitutional rights as citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The rural-dominated Alabama legislature consistently underfunded schools and services for the disenfranchised African Americans, but it did not relieve them of paying taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-SSpaces_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSpaces-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Partially as a response to chronic underfunding of education for African Americans in the South, the <a href="/wiki/Rosenwald_Fund" title="Rosenwald Fund">Rosenwald Fund</a> began funding the construction of what came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Rosenwald_School" title="Rosenwald School">Rosenwald Schools</a>. In Alabama, these schools were designed, and the construction partially financed with Rosenwald funds, which paid one-third of the construction costs. The fund required the local community and state to raise matching funds to pay the rest. Black residents effectively taxed themselves twice, by raising additional monies to supply matching funds for such schools, which were built in many rural areas. They often donated land and labor as well.<sup id="cite_ref-rosenwaldal_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosenwaldal-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mount_Sinai_School_Autauga_County_July_2011_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Mount_Sinai_School_Autauga_County_July_2011_1.jpg/220px-Mount_Sinai_School_Autauga_County_July_2011_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Mount_Sinai_School_Autauga_County_July_2011_1.jpg/330px-Mount_Sinai_School_Autauga_County_July_2011_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Mount_Sinai_School_Autauga_County_July_2011_1.jpg/440px-Mount_Sinai_School_Autauga_County_July_2011_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>The former <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai_School" title="Mount Sinai School">Mount Sinai School</a> in rural Autauga County, completed in 1919. It was one of the 387 <a href="/wiki/Rosenwald_Schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Rosenwald Schools">Rosenwald Schools</a> built in the state.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Beginning in 1913, the first 80 Rosenwald Schools were built in Alabama for African American children. A total of 387 schools, seven teachers' houses, and several vocational buildings were completed by 1937 in the state. Several of the <a href="/wiki/The_Rosenwald_School_Building_Fund_and_Associated_Buildings_Multiple_Property_Submission" title="The Rosenwald School Building Fund and Associated Buildings Multiple Property Submission">surviving school buildings</a> in the state are now listed on the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rosenwaldal_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rosenwaldal-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Continued racial discrimination and <a href="/wiki/Lynching" title="Lynching">lynchings</a>, agricultural depression, and the failure of the cotton crops due to <a href="/wiki/Boll_weevil" title="Boll weevil">boll weevil</a> infestation led tens of thousands of African Americans from rural Alabama and other states to seek opportunities in northern and midwestern cities during the early decades of the 20th century as part of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Great Migration (African American)">Great Migration</a> out of the South.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Reflecting this emigration, the population growth rate in Alabama (see "historical populations" table below) dropped by nearly half from 1910 to 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-census_data_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census_data-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the same time, many rural people migrated to the city of Birmingham to work in new industrial jobs. Birmingham experienced such rapid growth it was called the "Magic City".<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> By 1920, Birmingham was the 36th-largest city in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Heavy industry and mining were the basis of its economy. Its residents were under-represented for decades in the state legislature, which refused to redistrict after each decennial census according to population changes, as it was required by the state constitution. This did not change until the late 1960s following a lawsuit and court order.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1211633275">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Beginning in the 1940s, when the courts started taking the first steps to recognize the voting rights of black voters, the Alabama legislature took several counter-steps designed to disfranchise black voters. The legislature passed, and the voters ratified [as these were mostly white voters], a state constitutional amendment that gave local registrars greater latitude to disqualify voter registration applicants. Black citizens in Mobile successfully challenged this amendment as a violation of the <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifteenth Amendment</a>. The legislature also changed the boundaries of <a href="/wiki/Tuskegee,_Alabama" title="Tuskegee, Alabama">Tuskegee</a> to a 28-sided figure designed to fence out blacks from the city limits. The Supreme Court unanimously held that this racial "<a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering" title="Gerrymandering">gerrymandering</a>" violated the Constitution. In 1961,<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... the Alabama legislature also intentionally diluted the effect of the black vote by instituting numbered place requirements for local elections.<sup id="cite_ref-vra_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vra-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Industrial development related to the demands of World War II brought a level of prosperity to the state not seen since before the civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-SSpaces_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSpaces-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Rural workers poured into the largest cities in the state for better jobs and a higher standard of living. One example of this massive influx of workers occurred in Mobile. Between 1940 and 1943, more than 89,000 people moved into the city to work for war-related industries.<sup id="cite_ref-thomason2_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thomason2-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Cotton and other <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crops</a> faded in importance as the state developed a manufacturing and service base.
</p><p>Despite massive population changes in the state from 1901 to 1961, the rural-dominated legislature refused to reapportion House and Senate seats based on population, as required by the state constitution to follow the results of decennial censuses. They held on to old representation to maintain political and economic power in agricultural areas. One result was that <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_County,_Alabama" title="Jefferson County, Alabama">Jefferson County</a>, containing Birmingham's industrial and economic powerhouse, contributed more than one-third of all tax revenue to the state, but did not receive a proportional amount in services. Urban interests were consistently underrepresented in the legislature. A 1960 study noted that because of rural domination, "a minority of about 25% of the total state population is in majority control of the Alabama legislature."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pjhwpa_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pjhwpa-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States Supreme Court cases of <i><a href="/wiki/Baker_v._Carr" title="Baker v. Carr">Baker v. Carr</a></i> (1962) and <i><a href="/wiki/Reynolds_v._Sims" title="Reynolds v. Sims">Reynolds v. Sims</a></i> (1964), the court ruled that the principle of "<a href="/wiki/One_man,_one_vote" title="One man, one vote">one man, one vote</a>" needed to be the basis of both houses of state legislatures, and that their districts had to be based on population rather than geographic counties.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>African Americans continued to press in the 1950s and 1960s to end disenfranchisement and segregation in the state through the civil rights movement, including legal challenges. In 1954, the U.S. Supreme Court ruled in <i><a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown v. Board of Education</a></i> that public schools had to be <a href="/wiki/Desegregation_in_the_United_States" title="Desegregation in the United States">desegregated</a>, but Alabama was slow to comply. During the 1960s, under Governor <a href="/wiki/George_Wallace" title="George Wallace">George Wallace</a>, Alabama resisted compliance with federal demands for desegregation.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The civil rights movement had notable events in Alabama, including the <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_bus_boycott" title="Montgomery bus boycott">Montgomery bus boycott</a> (1955–1956), <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Rides" class="mw-redirect" title="Freedom Rides">Freedom Rides</a> in 1961, and 1965 Selma to Montgomery marches.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> These contributed to Congressional passage and enactment of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a> by the U.S. Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-cra64_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cra64-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Legal segregation ended in the states in 1964, but Jim Crow customs often continued until specifically challenged in court.<sup id="cite_ref-USDOJ_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJ-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, by 2017, many of Alabama's African Americans were living in Alabama's cities such as Birmingham and Montgomery. Also, the Black Belt region across central Alabama "is home to largely poor counties that are predominantly African-American. These counties include Dallas, <a href="/wiki/Lowndes_County,_Alabama" title="Lowndes County, Alabama">Lowndes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marengo_County,_Alabama" title="Marengo County, Alabama">Marengo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perry_County,_Alabama" title="Perry County, Alabama">Perry</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_2017_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_2017-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1972, for the first time since 1901, the legislature completed the congressional redistricting based on the decennial census. This benefited the urban areas that had developed, as well as all in the population who had been underrepresented for more than sixty years.<sup id="cite_ref-pjhwpa_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pjhwpa-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Other changes were made to implement representative state house and senate districts.
</p><p>Alabama has made some changes since the late 20th century and has used new types of voting to increase representation. In the 1980s, an omnibus redistricting case, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dillard_v._Crenshaw_County&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dillard v. Crenshaw County (page does not exist)">Dillard v. Crenshaw County</a></i>, challenged the <a href="/wiki/At-large" title="At-large">at-large</a> voting for representative seats of 180 Alabama jurisdictions, including counties and school boards. At-large voting had diluted the votes of any minority in a county, as the majority tended to take all seats. Despite African Americans making up a significant minority in the state, they had been unable to elect any representatives in most of the at-large jurisdictions.<sup id="cite_ref-vra_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vra-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As part of settlement of this case, five Alabama cities and counties, including <a href="/wiki/Chilton_County,_Alabama" title="Chilton County, Alabama">Chilton County</a>, adopted a system of <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">cumulative voting</a> for election of representatives in multi-seat jurisdictions. This has resulted in more proportional representation for voters. In another form of proportional representation, 23 jurisdictions use <a href="/wiki/Limited_voting" title="Limited voting">limited voting</a>, as in <a href="/wiki/Conecuh_County,_Alabama" title="Conecuh County, Alabama">Conecuh County</a>. In 1982, limited voting was first tested in Conecuh County. Together use of these systems has increased the number of African Americans and women being elected to local offices, resulting in governments that are more representative of their citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-cum_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cum-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in the 1960s, the state's economy shifted away from its traditional lumber, steel, and textile industries because of increased foreign competition. Steel jobs, for instance, declined from 46,314 in 1950 to 14,185 in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> However, the state, particularly Huntsville, benefited from the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">George C. Marshall Space Flight Center</a> in 1960, a major facility in the development of the Saturn rocket program and the space shuttle. Technology and manufacturing industries, such as automobile assembly, replaced some the state's older industries in the late twentieth century, but the state's economy and growth lagged behind other states in the area, such as Georgia and Florida.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="21st_century">21st century</h3></div>
<p>In 2001, Alabama Supreme Court chief justice <a href="/wiki/Roy_Moore" title="Roy Moore">Roy Moore</a> installed a statue of the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a> in the capitol in Montgomery. In 2002, the 11th US Circuit Court ordered the statue removed, but Moore refused to follow the court order, which led to protests around the capitol in favor of keeping the monument. The monument was removed in August 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A few natural disasters have occurred in the state in the twenty-first century. In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Ivan" title="Hurricane Ivan">Hurricane Ivan</a>, a category 3 storm upon landfall, struck the state and caused over $18&#160;billion of damage. It was among the most destructive storms to strike the state in its modern history.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/2011_Super_Outbreak" title="2011 Super Outbreak">super outbreak</a> of 62 tornadoes hit the state in April 2011 and killed 238 people, devastating many communities.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Geography">Geography</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Alabama" title="Geography of Alabama">Geography of Alabama</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Alabama_counties" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Alabama counties">List of Alabama counties</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Alabama" title="Geology of Alabama">Geology of Alabama</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Alabama_terrain_NA.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Map_of_Alabama_terrain_NA.jpg/220px-Map_of_Alabama_terrain_NA.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="364" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Map_of_Alabama_terrain_NA.jpg/330px-Map_of_Alabama_terrain_NA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Map_of_Alabama_terrain_NA.jpg/440px-Map_of_Alabama_terrain_NA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="810" data-file-height="1340" /></a><figcaption>Map of Alabama from the <a href="/wiki/National_Atlas_of_the_United_States" title="National Atlas of the United States">National Atlas of the United States</a> (2007)</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:OnoIslandAl.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/OnoIslandAl.jpg/220px-OnoIslandAl.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="218" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/OnoIslandAl.jpg/330px-OnoIslandAl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/OnoIslandAl.jpg/440px-OnoIslandAl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="714" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Ono_Island,_Alabama" title="Ono Island, Alabama">Ono Island</a> in Baldwin County</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:MonteSanoStateParkhsval.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/MonteSanoStateParkhsval.jpg/220px-MonteSanoStateParkhsval.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="121" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/MonteSanoStateParkhsval.jpg/330px-MonteSanoStateParkhsval.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/MonteSanoStateParkhsval.jpg/440px-MonteSanoStateParkhsval.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1072" data-file-height="590" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Monte_Sano_State_Park" title="Monte Sano State Park">Monte Sano State Park</a> in Huntsville</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cathedral_Cavern_2019.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Cathedral_Cavern_2019.png/220px-Cathedral_Cavern_2019.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Cathedral_Cavern_2019.png/330px-Cathedral_Cavern_2019.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Cathedral_Cavern_2019.png/440px-Cathedral_Cavern_2019.png 2x" data-file-width="5472" data-file-height="3648" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Cathedral_Caverns_State_Park" title="Cathedral Caverns State Park">Cathedral Caverns</a> in <a href="/wiki/Marshall_County,_Alabama" title="Marshall County, Alabama">Marshall County</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Alabama is the thirtieth-largest state in the United States with 52,419 square miles (135,760&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of total area: 3.2% of the area is water, making Alabama 23rd in the amount of surface water, also giving it the second-largest inland waterway system in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> About three-fifths of the land area is part of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coastal_Plain" title="Gulf Coastal Plain">Gulf Coastal Plain</a>, a gentle plain with a general descent towards the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> and the Gulf of Mexico. The <a href="/wiki/North_Alabama" title="North Alabama">North Alabama</a> region is mostly mountainous, with the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_River" title="Tennessee River">Tennessee River</a> cutting a large valley and creating numerous creeks, streams, rivers, mountains, and lakes.<sup id="cite_ref-NetState_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NetState-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alabama is bordered by the states of Tennessee to the north, Georgia to the east, Florida to the south, and Mississippi to the west. Alabama has coastline at the Gulf of Mexico, in the extreme southern edge of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-NetState_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NetState-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The state ranges in elevation from sea level<sup id="cite_ref-usgs_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> at Mobile Bay to more than 2,000 feet (610&#160;m) in the <a href="/wiki/Sand_Mountain_(Alabama)" title="Sand Mountain (Alabama)">northeast</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Cheaha_Mountain" title="Cheaha Mountain">Mount Cheaha</a><sup id="cite_ref-NetState_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NetState-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> at 2,413&#160;ft (735&#160;m).<sup id="cite_ref-ngs_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ngs-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alabama's land consists of 22&#160;million acres (89,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of forest or 67% of the state's total land area.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Suburban <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_County,_Alabama" title="Baldwin County, Alabama">Baldwin County</a>, along the Gulf Coast, is the largest county in the state in both land area and water area.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:NaturalBridgeAl.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/NaturalBridgeAl.jpg/220px-NaturalBridgeAl.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/NaturalBridgeAl.jpg/330px-NaturalBridgeAl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/NaturalBridgeAl.jpg/440px-NaturalBridgeAl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="720" /></a><figcaption>The Natural Bridge Rock in <a href="/wiki/Winston_County,_Alabama" title="Winston County, Alabama">Winston County</a> is the longest natural bridge east of the Rockies.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Areas in Alabama administered by the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a> include <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_Bend_National_Military_Park" title="Horseshoe Bend National Military Park">Horseshoe Bend National Military Park</a> near <a href="/wiki/Alexander_City,_Alabama" title="Alexander City, Alabama">Alexander City</a>; <a href="/wiki/Little_River_Canyon_National_Preserve" title="Little River Canyon National Preserve">Little River Canyon National Preserve</a> near <a href="/wiki/Fort_Payne,_Alabama" title="Fort Payne, Alabama">Fort Payne</a>; <a href="/wiki/Russell_Cave_National_Monument" title="Russell Cave National Monument">Russell Cave National Monument</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bridgeport,_Alabama" title="Bridgeport, Alabama">Bridgeport</a>; <a href="/wiki/Tuskegee_Airmen_National_Historic_Site" title="Tuskegee Airmen National Historic Site">Tuskegee Airmen National Historic Site</a> in Tuskegee; and <a href="/wiki/Tuskegee_Institute_National_Historic_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuskegee Institute National Historic Site">Tuskegee Institute National Historic Site</a> near Tuskegee.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, Alabama has four <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Forest" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Forest">National Forests</a>: <a href="/wiki/Conecuh_National_Forest" title="Conecuh National Forest">Conecuh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Talladega_National_Forest" title="Talladega National Forest">Talladega</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuskegee_National_Forest" title="Tuskegee National Forest">Tuskegee</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_B._Bankhead_National_Forest" title="William B. Bankhead National Forest">William B. Bankhead</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Alabama also contains the <a href="/wiki/Natchez_Trace_Parkway" title="Natchez Trace Parkway">Natchez Trace Parkway</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Selma_To_Montgomery_National_Historic_Trail" class="mw-redirect" title="Selma To Montgomery National Historic Trail">Selma To Montgomery National Historic Trail</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears National Historic Trail</a>.
</p><p>Natural wonders include the <a href="/wiki/Natural_Bridge,_Alabama" title="Natural Bridge, Alabama">"Natural Bridge"</a> rock, the longest <a href="/wiki/Natural_arch" title="Natural arch">natural bridge</a> east of the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rockies</a>, just south of <a href="/wiki/Haleyville,_Alabama" title="Haleyville, Alabama">Haleyville</a>; <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_Caverns_State_Park" title="Cathedral Caverns State Park">Cathedral Caverns</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Marshall_County,_Alabama" title="Marshall County, Alabama">Marshall County</a>, named for its cathedral-like appearance, which features one of the largest cave entrances and one of the largest stalagmites in the world; Ecor Rouge, in <a href="/wiki/Fairhope,_Alabama" title="Fairhope, Alabama">Fairhope</a>, the highest coastline point between <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> and Mexico;<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/DeSoto_Caverns" title="DeSoto Caverns">DeSoto Caverns</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Childersburg,_Alabama" title="Childersburg, Alabama">Childersburg</a>, the first officially recorded cave in the United States;<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Noccalula_Falls_Park" title="Noccalula Falls Park">Noccalula Falls</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Gadsden,_Alabama" title="Gadsden, Alabama">Gadsden</a>, which has a 90-foot waterfall; <a href="/wiki/Dismals_Canyon" title="Dismals Canyon">Dismals Canyon</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Phil_Campbell,_Alabama" title="Phil Campbell, Alabama">Phil Campbell</a>, which is home to two waterfalls and six natural bridges and is said to have been a hideout of <a href="/wiki/Jesse_James" title="Jesse James">Jesse James</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Stephens Gap Cave, in <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Alabama" title="Jackson County, Alabama">Jackson County</a>, which has a 143-foot pit and two waterfalls and is one of the most photographed wild cave scenes in America;<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Little River Canyon, near Fort Payne, one of the nation's longest mountaintop rivers; <a href="/wiki/Rickwood_Caverns_State_Park" title="Rickwood Caverns State Park">Rickwood Caverns</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Warrior,_Alabama" title="Warrior, Alabama">Warrior</a>, which has an underground pool, blind cave-fish, and 260-million-year-old limestone formations; and the <a href="/wiki/The_Walls_of_Jericho_(canyon)" title="The Walls of Jericho (canyon)">Walls of Jericho</a> canyon, on the Alabama–Tennessee border.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cliffs_inside_the_Wetumpka_impact_crater_in_Wetumpka,_Alabama.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Cliffs_inside_the_Wetumpka_impact_crater_in_Wetumpka%2C_Alabama.jpg/220px-Cliffs_inside_the_Wetumpka_impact_crater_in_Wetumpka%2C_Alabama.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="107" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Cliffs_inside_the_Wetumpka_impact_crater_in_Wetumpka%2C_Alabama.jpg/330px-Cliffs_inside_the_Wetumpka_impact_crater_in_Wetumpka%2C_Alabama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Cliffs_inside_the_Wetumpka_impact_crater_in_Wetumpka%2C_Alabama.jpg/440px-Cliffs_inside_the_Wetumpka_impact_crater_in_Wetumpka%2C_Alabama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1316" data-file-height="640" /></a><figcaption>Cliffs at the rim of the <a href="/wiki/Wetumpka_crater" title="Wetumpka crater">Wetumpka meteorite crater</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>A 5-mile (8&#160;km)-wide meteorite impact crater is located in <a href="/wiki/Elmore_County,_Alabama" title="Elmore County, Alabama">Elmore County</a>, just north of Montgomery. This is the <a href="/wiki/Wetumpka_crater" title="Wetumpka crater">Wetumpka crater</a>, the site of "Alabama's greatest natural disaster". A 1,000-foot (300&#160;m)-wide meteorite hit the area about 80 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-mlvguh_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mlvguh-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The hills just east of downtown <a href="/wiki/Wetumpka,_Alabama" title="Wetumpka, Alabama">Wetumpka</a> showcase the eroded remains of the impact crater that was blasted into the bedrock, with the area labeled the Wetumpka crater or astrobleme ("star-wound") because of the concentric rings of fractures and zones of shattered rock that can be found beneath the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, Christian Koeberl with the Institute of Geochemistry University of Vienna published evidence and established the site as the 157th recognized impact crater on Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Climate">Climate</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Alabama" title="Climate of Alabama">Climate of Alabama</a></div>
<p>The state is classified as <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical</a> (<i>Cfa</i>) under the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Koppen Climate Classification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The average annual temperature is 64<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>°F (18<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>°C). Temperatures tend to be warmer in the southern part of the state with its proximity to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, while the northern parts of the state, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachian Mountains</a> in the northeast, tend to be slightly cooler.<sup id="cite_ref-cprgsw_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cprgsw-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Generally, Alabama has very hot summers and mild winters with copious precipitation throughout the year. Alabama receives an average of 56 inches (1,400&#160;mm) of rainfall annually and enjoys a lengthy growing season of up to 300 days in the southern part of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-cprgsw_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cprgsw-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Summers in Alabama are among the hottest in the U.S., with high temperatures averaging over 90&#160;°F (32&#160;°C) throughout the summer in some parts of the state. Alabama is also prone to <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical storms and hurricanes</a>. Areas of the state far away from the Gulf are not immune to the effects of the storms, which often dump tremendous amounts of rain as they move inland and weaken.
</p><p>South Alabama reports many <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a>. The Gulf Coast, around Mobile Bay, averages between 70 and 80 days per year with thunder reported. This activity decreases somewhat further north in the state, but even the far north of the state reports thunder on about 60 days per year. Occasionally, thunderstorms are severe with frequent <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> and large <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>; the central and northern parts of the state are most vulnerable to this type of storm. Alabama ranks ninth in the number of deaths from lightning and tenth in the number of deaths from lightning strikes per capita.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Phil_Campbell_tornado_damage.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Phil_Campbell_tornado_damage.jpg/220px-Phil_Campbell_tornado_damage.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Phil_Campbell_tornado_damage.jpg/330px-Phil_Campbell_tornado_damage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Phil_Campbell_tornado_damage.jpg/440px-Phil_Campbell_tornado_damage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="675" /></a><figcaption>Tornado damage in <a href="/wiki/Phil_Campbell,_Alabama" title="Phil Campbell, Alabama">Phil Campbell</a> following the statewide <a href="/wiki/2011_Super_Outbreak" title="2011 Super Outbreak">April 27, 2011, tornado outbreak</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Alabama, along with <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, has the most confirmed <a href="/wiki/Fujita_scale" title="Fujita scale">F5</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_Fujita_scale" title="Enhanced Fujita scale">EF5</a> tornadoes of any state, according to statistics from the <a href="/wiki/National_Climatic_Data_Center" title="National Climatic Data Center">National Climatic Data Center</a> for the period January 1, 1950, to June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Several long-tracked F5/EF5 tornadoes have contributed to Alabama reporting more tornado fatalities since 1950 than any other state. The state was affected by the <a href="/wiki/1974_Super_Outbreak" title="1974 Super Outbreak">1974 Super Outbreak</a> and was devastated tremendously by the 2011 Super Outbreak. The 2011 Super Outbreak produced a record amount of tornadoes in the state. The tally reached 62.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The peak season for tornadoes varies from the northern to southern parts of the state. Alabama is one of the few places in the world that has a secondary tornado season in November and December besides the typically severe spring. The northern part—along the Tennessee River Valley—is most vulnerable. The area of Alabama and Mississippi most affected by tornadoes is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Dixie_Alley" title="Dixie Alley">Dixie Alley</a>, as distinct from the <a href="/wiki/Tornado_Alley" title="Tornado Alley">Tornado Alley</a> of the Southern Plains.
</p><p>Winters are generally mild in Alabama, as they are throughout most of the Southeastern United States, with average January low temperatures around 40&#160;°F (4&#160;°C) in Mobile and around 32&#160;°F (0&#160;°C) in Birmingham. Although snow is a rare event in much of Alabama, areas of the state north of Montgomery may receive a dusting of snow a few times every winter, with an occasional moderately heavy snowfall every few years. Historic snowfall events include <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Eve_1963_snowstorm" title="New Year&#39;s Eve 1963 snowstorm">New Year's Eve 1963 snowstorm</a> and the <a href="/wiki/1993_Storm_of_the_Century" title="1993 Storm of the Century">1993 Storm of the Century</a>. The annual average snowfall for the Birmingham area is 2 inches (51&#160;mm) per year. In the southern Gulf coast, snowfall is less frequent, sometimes going several years without any snowfall.
</p><p>Alabama's highest temperature of 112&#160;°F (44&#160;°C) was recorded on September 5, 1925, in the unincorporated community of <a href="/wiki/Centerville,_Alabama" title="Centerville, Alabama">Centerville</a>. The record low of −27&#160;°F (−33&#160;°C) occurred on January 30, 1966, in <a href="/wiki/New_Market,_Alabama" title="New Market, Alabama">New Market</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
